    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_353609264">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/353609264" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3712181-stephen-m" class="left imgcol" title="Stephen M"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409809404p2/3712181.jpg" alt="Stephen M" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/353609264?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 24, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3712181-stephen-m" class="user" title="Stephen M">Stephen M</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7597.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="Paperback " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">solitude is bliss</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Stephen M by: </span>
      The most beautiful girl at school
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3712181-stephen-m?shelf=metafictive-madness" class="actionLinkLite">metafictive-madness</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3712181-stephen-m?shelf=ocean-of-noise" class="actionLinkLite">ocean-of-noise</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3712181-stephen-m?shelf=thinking-of-a-dream-i-had" class="actionLinkLite">thinking-of-a-dream-i-had</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer353609264">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15047316180914476341">Many years later, as the most beautiful girl in town disclosed <em>the book</em> from her folded arms and revealed its brilliant glow to his eyes, Francisco Rodriguez de la Campiña was to recall that distant, savage summer when his grandmother first taught him to read. At that time, he would spend hours under the cockspur coral tree behind their bark and leaf house while his grandmother, Pilar Popa, lectured him on the finer points of grammatical etiquette. Peering over his shoulder, grasping his elbows</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15047316180914476341">Many years later, as the most beautiful girl in town disclosed <em>the book</em> from her folded arms and revealed its brilliant glow to his eyes, Francisco Rodriguez de la Campiña was to recall that distant, savage summer when his grandmother first taught him to read. At that time, he would spend hours under the cockspur coral tree behind their bark and leaf house while his grandmother, Pilar Popa, lectured him on the finer points of grammatical etiquette. Peering over his shoulder, grasping his elbows with her leathery hands, she would adjust the way in which he held his book: upper arms parallel to his body, forearms bent at a forty-five degree angle, his back stock straight. Fransisco Rodriguez never wanted to read and held onto a particular rancor for what his grandmother made him do every morning. His bitterness, however, extended well beyond his grandmother. For he held nearly every other person in town in a particular contempt. The kind that was reserved for those who deserved it, as Francisco believed. This thought, a complex cocktail of hurt feelings and indignation, was born out of Francisco’s ostracism and estrangement from his immediate family. All occurring well before Francisco came of age, it was set in motion, Francisco reasoned, long before the gypsies had first discovered that wide clearing in the woods that would become the town <em>Lugar Agradable</em>. Surely it was sown within the fabric of the universe by the great creator Himself the curse that would afflict and destroy Francisco’s life, that long causal chain of events, rippling through time and history that would bring about the specific genetic mutations among the alleles of his parents that were to become his deformities. Yes, God had a bone to pick with the soul of Francisco Rodriguez and he was the unfortunate victim caught within the whims of a malicious and manipulative deity. Pilar Popa took it upon herself to beat such self-aggrandizing nonsense out of her grandson. <br /><br />It began with a belt and a reinforcement of vocabulary, <em>¿herméticamente?</em> she asked him one morning’s lesson. Francisco’s eyes looked toward the ground. His grandmother snapped the two strips of her leather belt together. At the sound of the firm crack and her grandmother’s piercing voice, <em>Eyes on me!</em> Francisco looked up at his grandmother, tears already leaking out of the corners of his eyes. She had given him a long list of words and her oldest dictionary the previous night, but Francisco had shirked off his homework. He had opted instead to stare out of the hole in his bark wall, the one that overlooked the neighbor’s yard. For the most beautiful girl in town lived in that yard and Francisco would watch her with a grin spread across his face, content in this voyeuristic venture. Her name was Alejandra Hermosa and she tended to her family’s garden. She grew enormous eggplants that fed her many siblings—seven brothers and five sisters. Alejandra hardly spoke, especially when her father came outside to observe her work. She would only nod demurely to her father’s instructions and continue to work the soil. Her fingers delicately patted the ground around the stems of the plants, after pouring the proper amount of water she fetched from the town well. Whenever a plant began to shake and glow that off-white, yellow color of a ripe eggplant, she would gently remove it from its stem before it would shake itself overripe and withered. Francisco loved to watch the way she took care of the eggplant, with love and care. Never to rush her work, she drew upon some deep reserve of patience with her every action, laying out huge stockpiles of eggplants everyday to feed her siblings. <br /><br />Caught in the recesses of present moment’s regret, Francisco could only think of Alejandra and the way she took care of her eggplants. His daydream was shattered when his grandmother screamed and slammed her hand down on the table. She asked once more <em>¿herméticamente?</em> Francisco did not speak. He turned away from her and presented his bare bottom to her anticipating hands, wrapping the leather belt around them. His grandmother brought the belt across his cheeks. His whole body convulsed from the force of impact. The belt left its stinging bite every time. No matter how many forgotten words Francisco earned lashings for, the pain never lessened. Thankfully, Pilar Popa found two hits to be sufficient in disciplining her grandson. Francisco turned back to face her, leaning all weight on his thigh when he sat. The bruises on his bottom would never heal, as any dark blue of old bruises discolored, fresh ones would readily take their place. Pilar looked into his eyes that were sunken into their sockets from tears and felt a pang of pity for the boy. His entire body was a broken display begging for pity: his right leg splayed crooked in the grass, his miniature nose, two sizes too small, and lengthy oval face that was covered in blood-red sores and pockmarks. All of it guaranteed Francisco’s place as the biggest eyesore of the town. Few could look at the boy for long without feeling a great sadness plunge through their insides, an abstract mass of blackness that seated itself around the solar plexus. It would take a thorough cleansing at the river to wash the black sadness out of their system, as it slowly leaked out in osmotic diffusion from their skin. This same abstraction now, began to weasel its way into Pilar’s intestines. She disregarded it immediately. They had to begin his writing lessons.<br /><br />Over the years of Francisco’s tutelage, he was to labor over reams of parchment. Pilar designated time for him to study in his room where surely he would have learned to practice all the things she lectured on: how construct a proper sentence, abstaining from comma splices, knowing when to use a semi-colon when called for; he would have learned by then how to discriminate between using one sentence-lengthening punctuation mark over another, how to judge between the use of a period or a conjunction, never misspell a word, remember to capitalize every first word and proper noun, and learn the structure of any good sentence: simple subject/predicate form, compound, complex and compound-complex. But he did none of this. He didn’t look for a single moment at the reams of notes Abuela Pilar entrusted to him every night because he was spending all his time staring at Alejandra and the eggplants in her garden. What combination of sights, senses and emotions were coming to him when he peaked through that hole in his wall? He didn’t know. Nor did he spend much time to analyze these thoughts and compulsions coursing through him. All that he knew was that he was in raptures at the sight of her; he was in love with her, slayed by the mere recall of her face, her gentle grace. <br /><br />When the time came to prove his writing to Pilar, Francisco failed miserably. He began writing <em>Hoy es la día</em>. He only had to pause for a second before Francisco felt the blunt force of a hand beat the senses out of his head. He whimpered slightly and crossed it out. <em>
  <s>Hoy es la dia</s>
</em> and instead <em>Hoy es </em>el<em> día</em>. But as he wrote the next few words, they came out malformed. Her attempts to rectify his poor grammar came in fierce slaps upside his head whenever he made the slightest mistake. He had to write out, on the large leaves of a logwood tree, his and his grandmother’s name: <em>Yo soy Francisco Rodriguez de la Campiña. Mi abuela se llama Pilar Popa</em>. But his <em>jotas</em> got squibbly and they lost themselves across the page. His <em>ge</em> never found itself back to where it was supposed to, never making a perfect loop and his <em>hache</em> was pitiful, hardly resembling what it out to be. All his failures were met with punishment—Pilar’s ruthless hand and the shame of watching the logwood leaf crumble up and burn away with every grammatical error. <br /><br />All of this immediate punishment conditioned his mind to associate the written word with pain and embarrassment. It imbued an ingrained sense of paranoia with every letter he wrote. As each one landed on the surface of the logwood leaf, there was a twinge of uncertainty, as if each word was a condemnation of his soul, each one threatening to result in severe physical punishment. Whenever he would write forever after, it was never without a tightening in his shoulders, a queasy feeling in his stomach. It was that same feeling that was to manifest itself in every atom of his body, every fiber of his soul when he attempted a love poem addressed to the most beautiful girl in town, Alejandra Hermosa. He wrote the poem as by means of a two-pronged attack, on the one hand to express his gratitude for showing him the book that he loved so much and on the other to tell her how he could not live a single day without her, without seeing her carry her beautiful self from place to place, calming the stormy seas of his insides. All of which had rocked and turned in the pages of the book she had entrusted him. He would smile with every character description, laugh with the narrator and revel in the wealth of detail inside, associating every good memory with his thoughts about Alejandra Hermosa, ensuring the fact that if she would ever break his heart, the recall of any memory he had of the book would cull up all the emotional attachment he felt for Alejandra. He had spun and twisted her with the words of the book. If he spent enough time in introspection, he could see it inside of himself. All the memories labeled Alejandra Hermosa had found themselves in a tangled, knotted mess with the memories labeled <em>the book</em>. He frowned at the mess that love had made of his insides, the words flowing in and out of one another associated with the images of her face and her silky black hair that occasionally bobbed to the surface of that qualia pool. And every now and then, the dark clumps of knotted memories would form a blackened knot. Some disturbing black mass that portended only pain and anguish if potential heartbreak were to be on the horizon. The black knots threatened to metastasize and infect his heart and mind. Memories were what would surely crack open the delicate container of his mind when his sanity would flood out of the space it opened up. But he had to persevere. He had to tell Alejandra Hermosa what she truly meant to him. He ignored the tangle of memories and thought for a moment on the book.<br /><br />It was a magical one, one where dreams and reality had no distinction. The supreme would pop up in the mundane. Epiphanic realizations of meaning emerged from its labyrinthine paragraphs. Seemingly insurmountable gaps of time, easily bridged by inter-textual association that created the gestalt of beautiful significance. Francisco could not overcome the feeling of awe he felt every time he was submerged into the waters of the book’s words. Its ability to communicate some obfuscated human truth in its fractured reality. The book had its own logic that was influencing his mind, changing its structure. And all that he knew was that he must write the same, and give his words the same aphrodisiacal power that would force Alejandra Hermosa to love him.<br /><br />He began writing, slowly and painfully. Each word had to be forced out of his mind, like shoving putty through a pin-sized hole, only a spaghetti-strip of a word emerged from the overwhelming number of thoughts that were crammed into his mind. For every hour of thinking and toiling, only a sentence would emerge: <em>Tu eres el alero de mi vida / que salva la flor de mi corazon / desde la lluvia</em>. With each word dribbling slowly from his pen, he still felt that anxiety of creating the perfect sentence, the perfect word which would surely force her to love him. The image of Pilar Popa terrorized him then. Forever it would remain, in the background of his mind, to plague all his writing forever.<br /><br />When he finally finished, he had written across four full logwood leaves. He collected them together and set off to find Alejandra. There she was in her backyard. She was standing beneath her eggplants, as she always did for as long as Francisco could make memories. Her face was concentrated on one eggplant in particular that was making a giant fuss of itself. It was shaking mightily, about to rip out its insides and die right there on its branch before being plucked. Alejandra was quick to the draw as she pulled the eggplant off its branch before it could be finished. Francisco cleared his throat. He was standing only a few feet from her and Alejandra jumped from fright. She had only talked to this boy once before, when her mother told him that he had no friends and spent all his time alone in his room. She figured that she should be nice to the boy and that God sometimes wasn’t fair all the time to everyone. Pity is all that she felt for him and had figured that there was nothing quite like a book that helped fight loneliness. Otherwise, she kept her distance from him. He was deformed and hideous. Despite all the lectures her mother had given Alejandra on compassion and empathy, deep down, she was frightened and repulsed by him. Shortly after giving him the book, she limited all her interactions with him to the occasional moment of eye contact. He would catch sight of her from afar and she would busy herself with something else. But then there he was, standing only a few feet away. She was stuck. He was silent save for an intense breathing that was pulling his shoulders up and down in rapid succession. Anxiety heightened inside of her. She began to walk backwards, ready to call out for her father to come protect her. Francisco cursed his deformed body that was frozen in the immediacy of the moment, trying with all his might to pull his muscles and nerves into action. When the silence was broken, it was Alejandra’s voice, recoiling in self-defense <em>¿que quieres hijo?</em> Francisco was mumbling. All that Alejandra could make out was a muffled <em>no sé. no sé.</em> This lasted for about thirty seconds until Francisco, lifted the four leaves with his hand. She pulled them from his shaking fingers, saying <em>¿que es esto?</em>. Francisco didn’t even attempt an answer as he turned away and ran, limping on his crooked leg, back to the house.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15047316180914476341">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_294997196">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/294997196" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5960716-mala" class="left imgcol" title="Mala"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1435586901p2/5960716.jpg" alt="Mala" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/294997196?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5960716-mala" class="user" title="Mala">Mala</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="Hardcover 9780060531041" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=all-time-favourites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=classic-ever-enduring-appeal" class="actionLinkLite">classic-ever-enduring-appeal</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=dream-fantasy-horror" class="actionLinkLite">dream-fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=nobel-prize-winner" class="actionLinkLite">nobel-prize-winner</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=brain-pain-group-read" class="actionLinkLite">brain-pain-group-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer294997196">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9115431728613859307"><br /><br />"Sometimes great books have deleterious consequences for other writers, creating footsteps that can’t be walked in, shade the sun can’t penetrate, expectations that have no grounds. Gabriel García Márquez’s One Hundred Years of Solitude crushed the hopes of scores of young Colombian writers, and the spread of magic realism was not exactly beneficent, since it takes a magician to work magic and because rabbits don’t hide in just anybody’s hat."<br /><br />– William H Gass, in the essay 'Influence' from <a title="A Temple of Texts by William H. Gass" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156180.A_Temple_of_Texts">A Tem</a></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9115431728613859307"><br /><br />"Sometimes great books have deleterious consequences for other writers, creating footsteps that can’t be walked in, shade the sun can’t penetrate, expectations that have no grounds. Gabriel García Márquez’s One Hundred Years of Solitude crushed the hopes of scores of young Colombian writers, and the spread of magic realism was not exactly beneficent, since it takes a magician to work magic and because rabbits don’t hide in just anybody’s hat."<br /><br />– William H Gass, in the essay 'Influence' from <a title="A Temple of Texts by William H. Gass" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156180.A_Temple_of_Texts">A Temple of Texts</a>.<br /><br />This is a book of such terrible and heartbreaking beauty that I'm still reeling from the impact! Books like Nightwood &amp; One Hundred Years of Solitude are proof that greatness shdn't be judged by size alone. This tale is perfect cause in it Márquez finally found the "right tone"–<br /><br /><em> ...the tone that I eventually used in One Hundred Years of Solitude. It was based on the way my grandmother used to tell her stories. She told things that sounded supernatural and fantastic, but she told them with complete naturalness. When I finally discovered the tone I had to use, I sat down for eighteen months and worked every day</em>.*<br /><br />The mythical Macondo could be any place on earth where mankind was promised paradise but destroyed it as only man could.<br /><br />Although Márquis said that he only wrote this as a book about incest, it's quite clear that it is a metaphor for the political &amp; social history of Colombia rather broadly of Latin America's colonial past &amp; its tentative march towards modernity as most events described herein are based on facts: Márquez’s native town of Aracataca as the inspiration for the fictional Macondo, the long &amp; bloody civil war roiling South America 1850 onwards, the political assasinations, the arrival of the railways &amp; the cinema, the cruel exploitation of Colombia by the American United Fruit Company, &amp; the horrific massacre of the protesting workers by the Colombian military at the behest of the foreign imperialists, are some of the instances.<br /><br />"<em>García Márquez’s masterpiece, however, appeals not just to Latin American experiences, but to larger questions about human nature. It is, in the end, a novel as much about specific social and historical circumstances—disguised by fiction and fantasy—as about the possibility of love and the sadness of alienation and solitude</em>."<br /><br />Just as Rushdie described the waning years of the British Empire &amp; then a free India's tryst with destiny through the Sinai family in Midnight's Children ( a book inspired by this book!), the narrative here is told through the meteoric rise &amp; rise &amp; subsequent decline &amp; fall of the House of Buendias — the first family of Macondo who become a symbol of the culture &amp; the country.<br />Like the famous first families around the world – the Kennedys, the Perons, the Gandhis, the Bhuttos – their charisma carries their curse:<br /><br />The charismatic patriarch José Aureliano Buendia, who starts with such dreams &amp; promise, like so many of his descendants, eventually resigns himself:<br /><em> "We shall never get anywhere. . . . We'll rot our lives away here without the benefits of science". (19)<br />His descendants all inherit the same difficulty, and thus all eventually succumb to the power of nostalgia, to opting out of their historical reality, which they have never really understood clearly. They cope with their failure by an inner withdrawal...Loneliness in Macondo and among the Buendias is not an accidental condition, something that could be alleviated by better communications or more friends, and it is not the metaphysical loneliness of existentialists, a stage shared by all men. It is a particular vocation, a shape of character that is inherited, certainly, but also chosen, a doom that looks inevitable but is freely endorsed. The Buendias seek out their solitude, enclose themselves in it as if it were their shroud. As a result they become yet another emblem of the unreality</em>.**<br /><br /><strong>What's in a name? A lot, it seems! </strong><br /><br />The theme of a circular time is emphasised again &amp; again through many devices - The multi-generational Buendia family keep giving the same ancestral names over &amp; over to the children of the family, any attempt to break away from this practice is thwarted. The reduction in names' length means reduction in other ways as well – the boys are less of men - more dissolute, purposeless &amp; solitary. The Buendias put the D back in <strong>dysfunctional</strong> : incest, adultery, debauchery, self-centeredness &amp; excesses of all sorts abound. By having the same names they are condemned to repeat the mistakes of their earlier namesakes - first as farce then as tragedy; their ineffectual repetitive behaviour symbolised in the futile thirty-two armed uprisings &amp; the little gold fishes of Colonel Aureliano Buendia.<br /><br /><strong>The narrative plays out like a Greek tragedy – </strong> The characters seem fated to act out their lives as if there were no other way – for example, the seventeen boys of Colonel Aureliano, with the Ash Wednesday cross on their foreheads, are sitting ducks for political vendetta. The Biblical allusions are woven throughout – The Paradise discovered &amp; lost, the deluge &amp; plagues &amp; finally Macondo is so deep in sins that like Sodom &amp; Gomorrah, it has to be destroyed. The ending is heartbreaking but it couldn't have ended any other way. But if you read closely, there is a ray of hope!<br /><br />I can't recommend this book enough - the epic scope of its narrative, the Magical Realism that became a standard for others writing in this genre, the deeply flawed but oh so human &amp; memorable characters &amp; Márquez's exquisite &amp; at times hypnotic prose will keep you glued to this profoundly sad &amp; disturbing tale.<br /><br /><strong> A note regarding spoilers </strong><br /><br />Readers who are finicky about their spoiler alerts shd avoid this book – after every few pages the omniscient narrator gleefully announces the gruesome deaths that will befall the various members of the Buendia family, not to mention the back &amp; forth in narrative time, the predictions &amp; foreshadowing galore.<br /><strong> Point is, spoiler alerts are for ninnies – Adults just get on with it! </strong><br /><br />(*) Paris Review - The Art of Fiction No. 69, Gabriel Garcia Marquez<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.theparisreview.org/interviews/3196/the-art-of-fiction-no-69-gabriel-garcia-marquez" target="_blank">http://www.theparisreview.org/intervi...</a><br /><br />(**) A must read:<br />Lecture on One Hundred Years of Solitude<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://records.viu.ca/%7Ejohnstoi/introser/marquez.htm" target="_blank">http://records.viu.ca/~johnstoi/intro...</a><br /><br />This too:<br /><br />Memory and Prophecy, Illusion and Reality Are Mixed and Made to Look the Same<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.nytimes.com/books/97/06/15/reviews/marque-solitude.html" target="_blank">http://www.nytimes.com/books/97/06/15...</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9115431728613859307">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer293355342">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13922966334520254492">Ah!<br /><br />Has it really happened?<br /><br />Is it really a novel?<br /><br />It's one of those books which leave you with somewhat these kind of thoughts; it's a book which moves with every word. The novel deals with so many themes that it really hard to associate it with a few.<br /><br />However, one thing is for sure that the novel leaves you spellbound with an 'almost out of the world experience'; and you want to experience it just one more time every time you experience it !!! </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1134153427">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1134153427" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/32002534-gautam" class="left imgcol" title="Gautam"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1439471369p2/32002534.jpg" alt="Gautam" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1134153427?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 20, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/32002534-gautam" class="user" title="Gautam">Gautam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/930693.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="Paperback 9780141032436" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/32002534-gautam?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/32002534-gautam?shelf=magic-realism" class="actionLinkLite">magic-realism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1134153427">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13051122885410334484">Let me kickstart my review with a conversation I have had with one of my friends:<br /><br /><em>She: What you upto!?<br />Me: Nothing much! Just finished reading Marquez’s OHOS ! <br />She: Oh ! I know him … he is the one who writes nonsense beautifully.. (Says innocently)<br />Me: You don’t like him? (Evidently disconcerted at her reply)<br />She: umm yes... kind of !</em><br /><br />I frankly acquiesce to the fact that I took her statement literally at first, as the word 'nonsense' felt like an inextricable knot. But when I meditated upon it, I fou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13051122885410334484">Let me kickstart my review with a conversation I have had with one of my friends:<br /><br /><em>She: What you upto!?<br />Me: Nothing much! Just finished reading Marquez’s OHOS ! <br />She: Oh ! I know him … he is the one who writes nonsense beautifully.. (Says innocently)<br />Me: You don’t like him? (Evidently disconcerted at her reply)<br />She: umm yes... kind of !</em><br /><br />I frankly acquiesce to the fact that I took her statement literally at first, as the word 'nonsense' felt like an inextricable knot. But when I meditated upon it, I found her inadvertent statement rather true. Isn’t nonsense the same thing that doesn’t conform to our sense of reality? Isn’t nonsense the same word that you use to shrug-off actions/things that don’t belong to our ‘realistic’ world? So, when you think about it, nonsense is something that is not natural/realistic to our intelligence (something that doesn’t make sense) – It is therefore Preternatural in a broad way. The main ingredient in the genre ‘magic realism’ is the very preternatural element that one can simply call ‘Nonsense’ in terms of ‘reality’..<br /><br />Eventhough my friend meant nonsense to be plain no-sense, she used something along with it –Beautiful. My next question to myself was this: How could something nonsense be beautiful? (sounds like a disturbing oxymoron)<br /><br />My mind, conscience proffered the answer for the befuddling question in the following way : there is a cogent magical touch that makes even nonsense beautiful, that pushes you to believe in unreality, that prods you to swim in the shimmering waters of preter-naturalism, and that magical touch- it’s meaning- could be ascribed plainly to <strong>Writing</strong>. <br /><br />The persuasive writing of Marquez that impels one to get completely dissolved in a paranormal world (nonsense) ,that undo the ligation of your being with its adherence to reality in a piecemeal manner, and that makes the realism fanatics to get awestruck is the reason why ‘The Writing’ resembles closely to Kohinoor diamond in its elegance and beauty. It is true that the first 100 pages were quite hard to ingest as the translucent layer of reality that besieged me impeded the dazzling light of ‘magic realism’ that was bathing my whole being. But since the intensity of the light was so brilliant that the layer of reality was slowly dissolving to expose my being to its invigorating dazzle. As the pages rolled by, I began to enjoy swimming in the new waters of illusion – Waters of magic realism.<br /><br />According to me, the susceptibility to appreciate a novel like OHOS depends on the thickness of the layer of ‘reality’ that encapsulates you.(highly subjective)<br /><br />1.	People with a loose regenerative layer of reality over them which could dissolve and reappear – They can appreciate, enjoy, and contemplate matters outside reality(flexible readers).<br /><br />2.	People with a hard shell of reality that masks anything that is originated from outside of the same- They drop the book deeming it non-sense (scrupulous Naturalists).<br /><br /><strong>If Literature was a Jewelers Shop : A subjective analysis of Realism Vs Magic realism :-</strong><br /><br />Thank you for allowing me to make another digression. As this novel was my foray into magic realism, I tried to draw the differences between Imaginative realism and Realism. After the futile attempts to clarify intuition, my mind envisioned the differences in the form of a metaphor- a beaded jewellery. <br /><br />I imagined Literature to be a beaded necklace, where each of its beads corresponds to credibility of characters, believability of the plot, feasibility of events etc etc respectively.<br /><br />A perfect Realism Novel thus could be envisioned as a necklace consisting of beads of uniform size, color and rounded shape. It is the most ‘ideal’ form of necklace that is conceivable to our sense and which shows total justice to our judiciousness.<br /><br />A ‘perfect’ Novel of Magic realism, could be envisioned as a necklace with’ imperfect’ beads that are of amorphous shape, non-uniform size, and varied colors .Thus, while taking a gander at the beads individually, one may find it impossible and inconceivable to believe of its prospect in becoming a jewellery piece once stringed together. Thus many people lose interest without bearing patience to see the final product and return to being realism lovers, which is a safe zone. People who are patient enough to wait till the imperfect, incongruous beads are meticulously stringed together by a ‘masterful craftsman’ are sure to get the most beautiful necklace that is made out of the most nonsensical beads conceivable to our minds.<br /><br />So, In a nutshell ,without ranting, Magic realism is not meant to be scrutinized or admired as individual segments, but in its ‘entirety’ ; the genre ‘magic realism’ may seem nonsensical if perceived through its individual characteristics as what happens when one looks at the irregular beads individually of the very beautiful necklace. <br /><br /><strong>The key to its beauty (of the necklace and novel) lies in the alignment of these imperfections in a perfect order, in a masterful way that the very imperfections get faded away with the rectitude of perfect alignment.</strong><br /><br /><strong>Main Review:</strong><br /><br />It is indubitably difficult, as I already said, to write a meticulous review on a novel that proffers its beauty in its entirety. You may remember what my friend said about OHOS being a beautiful nonsense. This very nonsense, its meaning, could be ascribed to something else – <strong>unbridled imagination</strong>. Marquez adroitly painted an unprecedented settlement called macondo, which is solitary, remote and aloof from the bustling world of reality (forget about the metaphorical aspects of macondo and wars), and peopled it with imaginary, outlandish characters with imaginary, outlandish traits who live a life of imaginary, outlandish course. This Macondo and its occupants have no relation with our reality or our world, because it is a separate world and separate reality in its own way. <br /><br />OHOS tells about Macondo – its birth, proliferation, rise and decline. Expeditious and enterprising José Arcadio Buendia, who founded Macondo, is the starting point of this fervent saga of a family’s existence, life and solitude spanning over 100 years, ending with Aureliano Babilonia and his deciphering of enigmatic parchments. In Buendia family, time, as it seems, moves in a circle: the basic disposition and madness of the family members get bequeathed to subsequent generations without much alteration. This was evident in Ursula’s sudden outburst of revelation which was imbued with consternation:<br /><br /><em>The children inherit parents madness</em><br /><br />I frankly didn’t have difficulty in remembering all the Arcadios and Aurelianos , as I remembered them through their idiosyncrasies, which were singular. What I couldn’t keep abreast was the rolling years that moved like a speed boat in a misty night. I find it difficult to accurately perceive the ages of characters too as the story steers forward in a non-linear fashion meandering intermittently around the main track in the form of fornication and wars.<br /><br />Through unremitting chain of incest, inbreeding, and fornication, Buendia family continued its proliferation. The Aurelianos and Arcadios engendered from these inbreeding, philandering and ephemeral relationships had the shared traits and disposition of their parents, and the torrent of paranormal events that gushed about in the family over years like a whirling wind elicited ‘Nostalgia’ in them, which subsequently weighed down on their burdened hearts, and which eventually enmeshed them eternally in a web of solitude, and they inevitably reached the preternatural culmination of their lives. <br /><br />The paranormal death/ending of the characters is one of the riveting elements in the novel as in: Remedios the beauty levitating to the skies; Jose Arcadio being gunned down by an unknown entity and his blood traveling all the way to his mother; Rebecca succumbing to a solitary death in the ramshackle of a house after Jose Arcadio’s death; Amaranta through ephemeral clairvoyance foresees her death calculated precisely to the second; Amaranta Ursula’s death due to the incessant bleeding after her delivery; and finally the effacing of the Buendia family and Macondo,both from the world immediately after the deciphering of the Malequiades Parchments by Aureliano Babilonia has been accomplished.<br /><br />I know my main review turns out a flop due to my incapacity in writing a terse review or a meticulous analysis of a novel which could be described and admired only in its entirety. ‘The very Novel itself is a review of the Novel’ if I should say in Marquez’s style. To sum up my take on this book, Gabriel Garcia Marquez’s ‘One hundred Years of Solitude’ has been a novel experience for me as a reader and as an aspiring writer, and it has indomitably broadened my reading horizon to another level. I have read this book during one of the busiest and tension-filled periods of my life, and in all these days of turmoil I could proudly say that I only found solace and solitude in reading Marquez’s One Hundred Years of Solitude.<br /><br /><strong>5 stars on 5 !</strong><br /><br />-gautam<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13051122885410334484">...more</a>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3218741867790630602"><br />Well Mr Marquez may have a Nobel Prize for his mantelpiece and a pretty good imagination for writing what with the levitating women and babies made of ice cream but he has no imagination at all when he is thinking of his characters names which are like to drive you entirely insane in this novel, will you please look at this. There are five people called Arcadio, ,three ladies called Remedios, two ladies called Amaranta and there’s a Pietro and a Petra which look quite similar, and there are 23 people called Aureliano (17 of them sons of an Aureliano, so this father has as much lack of name imagination as Mr Marquez). It does give a reader brain ache trying to remember who is who and why they are levitating and which one lives to be 530 years old. I think this is a very good novel for people who like to go into trances for hours at a time. <br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_99258058">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99258058" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2447803-jason" class="left imgcol" title="Jason"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1246308632p2/2447803.jpg" alt="Jason" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/99258058?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 26, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2447803-jason" class="user" title="Jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7599.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="Paperback 9780061120091" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer99258058">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15949705041654811215">This book went from 5, to 4, to 3 stars. It went from brilliant &amp; zany, to unique &amp; amusing, to overworked &amp; predictable. Magical realism--the sine qua non of Gabriel Garcia Marquez, the archetype, the empyreal novel that pioneered the outburst of this type of South American writing. I would not re-read this novel, but I would recommend it to all who savor the radial expanse of genre in literature. To be considered a comprehensive reader at life's end, you will had to have read magic</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15949705041654811215">This book went from 5, to 4, to 3 stars. It went from brilliant &amp; zany, to unique &amp; amusing, to overworked &amp; predictable. Magical realism--the sine qua non of Gabriel Garcia Marquez, the archetype, the empyreal novel that pioneered the outburst of this type of South American writing. I would not re-read this novel, but I would recommend it to all who savor the radial expanse of genre in literature. To be considered a comprehensive reader at life's end, you will had to have read magical realism, and <em>One Hundred Years of Solitude</em> won the Nobel Prize.<br /><br />Ofttimes you experience works of art just to be exposed to something culture holds worthy, no matter the medium; music; painting; literature; dance; theater; technology. <strong>***BEGIN SOAPBOX***</strong>So you don't like Western movies; you still need to watch <em>Unforgiven</em>, the Best Movie of the Year 1992. So you don't like broad brush strokes; you still need to see in person a flower painted by Van Gogh. So you don't like 20th century plays; you still need to see enacted the vicious realism of Eugene O'Neal's <em>A Long Day's Journey Into Night</em>. So you don't like Rock-n-Roll; you still need to listen to the perfect album by Guns and Roses, <em>Appetite For Destruction</em>. You don't like warfare; you still need to take a tour of a Nimitz-class aircraft carrier, considered the most complicated man made structure ever. So you don't like jazz; you still......get the point.<strong>***END SOAPBOX***</strong><br /><br /><em>One Hundred Years of Solitude</em> needs to be experienced, then put back on the library rack. You've just been exposed to something the literary world holds worthy. It may not have been exactly my taste (like Garcia Marquez's 5 star <em>Love in the Time of Cholera</em>), but it was worthwhile in all its strangeness.<br /><br />Strangeness. How to define what I consider a strange novel? Let me try a visual representation or a visual transcription of how I would draw this novel. Why you ask? Well, why the hell not. <br /><br /><u>Contour drawing</u>--also known as continuous-line drawing, is an artistic technique used in the field of art in which the artist sketches the contour of a subject by drawing continuous lines that result in a drawing that is contorted and/or abstracted (wikipedia). 'Contorted and abstracted.' That's the key. Contour drawing is a type of art that uses one single line without lifting the pen from the paper, thereby creating a visual effect that is sometimes representative, sometimes complex, and sometimes contorted and/or abstracted. <u>Blind contour drawing</u>, a subset, is done without ever looking at the paper until the subject is complete, and is usually even more contorted and/or abstracted.<br /><br />To me, Marquez was creating a literary version of contour drawing. <em>One Hundred Years of Solitude</em> has the rudimentary look and feel of a novel. It's got all the right components, an epic story that follows 100 years of the Buendia family in the remote town of Macondo, Columbia. I believe Marquez sat at his typewriter, wrote the first sentence, took about 400 coffee breaks, but didn't stop until 417 pages later with the last sentence. The story is a single line never lifted from the paper. <br /><br />I don't think magical realism needs to be edited. It's a non-stop, brute-force narrative that keeps pushing forward, relentlessly, interminably, without a slack in pace. But this is not to say that the story is perfectly chronological. It isn't. Instead, like contour drawing, <em>One Hundred Years of Solitude</em>, is pages full of overlapping spirals and squiggles that keep moving slightly to the right, so that there's intersecting lines in a rhythmic doodle, always moving forward but causing us to cross through previous story over and over again, repeatedly, but never edited; a single line. You move forward in time, then move back in time, forward and back in circles. It's a brilliant &amp; zany technique that captures the same kind of storyline fable your grandmother may have told you when you were 7. Anything's possible in magical realism, but if you slow down and question the finer points, then you lose the bigger picture. Consequently, the story has a breathless quality. It's almost as if grandma slows down and thinks about the subject she's covered, then she will have lifted the pencil from the paper, and she will have lost her way, unable to recover any sense of the story; the magic will have dissipated.<br /><br />So, the story was pretty decent. But, after a couple hundred pages, the breathless, right-handed, overlapping spirals of storyline became overworked, as if every time grandma took a sip from her white wine, pushing way past bedtime, you could tell exactly how the story was about to continue. Not what the story was going to be, or what words were going to come from her mouth, but you could tell closely how she was going to say it.<br /><br />I recommend you read <em>One Hundred Years of Solitude</em> in the largest installments you can. If you read it over several weeks, or only a few pages at a time, you will absolutely--I guarantee--lose the gist. You'll find yourself going back and trying to figure out which of the 3 Jose Arcadios or 6 Aurelianos the story is discussing, and in which spiral. I had a permanent bookmark on page 0 that outlined the family genealogy.<br /><br />New words: organdy, joie de vivre, Babrant, proboscidian</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15949705041654811215">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_9483420">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9483420" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/289211-shira" class="left imgcol" title="Shira"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Shira" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/9483420?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 16, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/289211-shira" class="user" title="Shira">Shira</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="Hardcover 9780060531041" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer9483420'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer9483420">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10606167644854189953">Ok, I hate when this happens. I hear great things about a book for years, I finally decide to pick it up, and it turns out that I HATE it! It's not just that it's not my kind of book -- it's that I cannot for the life of me figure out what other people saw in this book. I found it to be utterly disturbing. I have never seen so much incest, adultery, rape, and bestiality in the same book! <br /><br />I know it's supposed to be magical realism, but the sum total of what happens to this family is just over the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10606167644854189953">Ok, I hate when this happens. I hear great things about a book for years, I finally decide to pick it up, and it turns out that I HATE it! It's not just that it's not my kind of book -- it's that I cannot for the life of me figure out what other people saw in this book. I found it to be utterly disturbing. I have never seen so much incest, adultery, rape, and bestiality in the same book! <br /><br />I know it's supposed to be magical realism, but the sum total of what happens to this family is just over the top. Let me give an example. There are 2 adopted sisters, Rebeca and Amaranta and both fall in love with the same guy, Pietro. Pietro in turn falls in love with Rebeca and they are engaged. Amaranta is so jealous that she tells Rebeca that she will never let the marriage happen. Amaranta does everything she can to delay the marriage, but finally it seems inevitable, so she decides to poison Rebeca. She doesn't really want to kill her sister, so she prays for something else to delay the wedding. Her prayer is answered when their sister-in-law dies, but then she feels so guilty because that wasn't the delay she had in mind. Meanwhile, Rebeca falls in love with and elopes with her adopted brother, who is really her cousin. After some time, Pietro now falls in love with Amaranta, but she is no longer interested. He kills himself in despair. She feels so guilty that she puts her hands in a burning stove and scars them for life. Later on, she and her nephew, whom she has raised from birth, fall in love. Well you get the idea. Lifetime movies have nothing on this book! :) </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10606167644854189953">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1257985041">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257985041" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="left imgcol" title="Lyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428721461p2/5253785.jpg" alt="Lyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1257985041?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="user" title="Lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="Hardcover 9780060531041" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1257985041">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17199986632204505002">Mystical and captivating.<br /><br />One Hundred Years of Solitude by Nobel laureate Gabriel García Márquez, first published in 1967 in his native Colombia and then first published in English in 1970, is a unique literary experience, overwhelming in its virtuosity and magnificent in scope.<br /><br />I recall my review of Tolstoy’s <a title="War and Peace by Leo Tolstoy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/656.War_and_Peace">War and Peace</a>, trying to describe a book like it and realizing there are no other books like it; it is practically a genre unto itself. That said, One Hundred Years of Solitude is a masterpi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17199986632204505002">Mystical and captivating.<br /><br />One Hundred Years of Solitude by Nobel laureate Gabriel García Márquez, first published in 1967 in his native Colombia and then first published in English in 1970, is a unique literary experience, overwhelming in its virtuosity and magnificent in scope.<br /><br />I recall my review of Tolstoy’s <a title="War and Peace by Leo Tolstoy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/656.War_and_Peace">War and Peace</a>, trying to describe a book like it and realizing there are no other books like it; it is practically a genre unto itself. That said, One Hundred Years of Solitude is a masterpiece of narrative ability, and is itself unique as a statement, but reminiscent of many other great books: Pasternak’s <a title="Doctor Zhivago by Boris Pasternak" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/130440.Doctor_Zhivago">Doctor Zhivago</a>, Lowry’s <a title="Under the Volcano by Malcolm Lowry" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31072.Under_the_Volcano">Under the Volcano</a>, Buck’s <a title="The Good Earth (House of Earth, #1) by Pearl S. Buck" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1078.The_Good_Earth__House_of_Earth___1_">The Good Earth</a>, and Joyce’s <a title="Ulysses by James Joyce" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses">Ulysses</a> were the works that I thought of while reading, but no doubt this is a one of a kind.<br /><br />Using all of the literary devices I have ever learned and making up many more as he went along, García Márquez established a new epoch of descriptive resonance. Magic realism and hyperbole abound in his fantastic history of the mythical town of Macondo separated by mountains and a swamp road from everything else and of the Buendía family, whose lifeblood was the dramatic heart of the village from inception until the fateful end.<br /><br />García Márquez employs incestuous and repetitive family situations to emphasize his chronicle and a dynamic characterization that is labyrinthine in its complexity. Dark humor walks the ancient halls of the ancestral mansion home along with the ghosts of those who have come before. Incredibly García Márquez ties it all together into a complete and prophetically sound ending that breathes like poetry to the finish.<br /><br />Finally I must concede that this review is wholly inadequate. This is a book that must be read.<br /><br /><img width="241" height="400" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448636869i/17169032.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17199986632204505002">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KgE43SDXzVegKezrlaG3hdMtVFYFt98n6VHjua88dXNP1QcO0gXZrSzxmFDrwFE3mtqXT0xqmI2DWOYrY6mDNg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In just the 23 FCE editions and reprintings, it had sold 1,143,000 copies by November 1997. Other editions in Mexico, Spain, and other nations have sold countless more copies. It is Rulfo\'s second book, after the short story collection El Llano en llamas, translated into English as The Burning Plain and other Stories. It has had a major influence in the development of magical realism and it is told in a mixture of first and third person narration. Gabriel García Márquez said that he had not felt like that since reading The Metamorphosis, while Jorge Luis Borges called it one of the best novels in literature.\n\nThe novel has been translated twice into English. The more recent translation is by Margaret Sayers Peden which has received numerous film adaptations. This chorus includes the muses of visceral realism, the beautiful Font sisters; their father, an architect interned in a Mexico City asylum; a sensitive young follower of Octavio Paz; a foul-mouthed American graduate student; a French girl with a taste for the Marquis de Sade; the great-granddaughter of Leon Trotsky; a Chilean stowaway with a mystical gift for numbers; the anorexic heiress to a Mexican underwear empire; an Argentinian photojournalist in Angola; and assorted hangers-on, detractors, critics, lovers, employers, vagabonds, real-life literary figures, and random acquaintances.\n\nA polymathic descendant of Borges and Pynchon, Roberto Bolaño traces the hidden connection between literature and violence in a world where national boundaries are fluid and death lurks in the shadow of the avant-garde.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez">About Gabriel Garcí­a Márquez</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez"><img alt="13450" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1408500613p2/13450.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer13480462591278818141">Gabriel José de la Concordia García Márquez was a Colombian novelist, short-story writer, screenwriter and journalist. García Márquez, familiarly known as "Gabo" in his native country, was considered one of the most significant authors of the 20th century. In 1982, he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature.<br /><br />He studied at the University of Bogotá and late worked as a reporter for the Colombian ne</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13480462591278818141">Gabriel José de la Concordia Garcí­a Márquez was a Colombian novelist, short-story writer, screenwriter and journalist. Garcí­a Márquez, familiarly known as "Gabo" in his native country, was considered one of the most significant authors of the 20th century. In 1982, he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature.<br /><br />He studied at the University of Bogotá and late worked as a reporter for the Colombian newspaper <em>El Espectador</em> and as a foreign correspondent in Rome, Paris, Barcelona, Caracas and New York. He wrote many acclaimed non-fiction works and short stories, but is best-known for his novels, such as <em>One Hundred Years of Solitude</em> (1967) and <em>Love in the Time of Cholera</em> (1985). His works have achieved significant critical acclaim and widespread commercial success, most notably for popularizing a literary style labeled as magical realism, which uses magical elements and events in order to explain real experiences. Some of his works are set in a fictional village called Macondo, and most of them express the theme of solitude.<br /><br />(Arabic: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5029628._">??????? ?????? ??????</a>) (Hebrew: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7091051._">?????? ?????? ????</a>) </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13480462591278818141">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3295655-cien-a-os-de-soledad">Quotes from One Hundred Years...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“It's enough for me to be sure that you and I exist at this moment.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/315847-it-s-enough-for-me-to-be-sure-that-you-and" class="actionLinkLite">3528 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“He dug so deeply into her sentiments that in search of interest he found love, because by trying to make her love him he ended up falling in love with her. Petra Cotes, for her part, loved him more and more as she felt his love increasing, and that was how in the ripeness of autumn she began to believe once more in the youthful superstition that poverty was the servitude of love. Both looked back then on the wild revelry, the gaudy wealth, and the unbridled fornication as an annoyance and they lamented that it had cost them so much of their lives to find the paradise of shared solitude. Madly in love after so many years of sterile complicity, they enjoyed the miracle of living each other as much at the table as in bed, and they grew to be so happy that even when they were two worn-out people they kept on blooming like little children and playing together like dogs.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/8238-he-dug-so-deeply-into-her-sentiments-that-in-search" class="actionLinkLite">1397 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
